<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/templates/jsp/pb2/js/plugin/thiss-ds.js" nonce="616014b6414840f0b5c384fa6ecc976c"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="616014b6414840f0b5c384fa6ecc976c">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '484a7fe8-e1dc-4266-8414-15c952004a95'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

            function riskifiedBeaconLoad() {

                s.src = url;
                var x = document.getElementsByTagName('script')[0];
                x.parentNode.insertBefore(s, x);
            }

                window.attachEvent('onload', riskifiedBeaconLoad)

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d41595908" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are widely considered a hopeful alternative to lithium-ion battery technology. However, they still face challenges, such as low rate capability, unsatisfactory cycling stability, and inferior variable-temperature performance. In this study, a hierarchical Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>3</sub> (NVPF) @reduced graphene oxide (rGO)/carbon nanotube&nbsp;(CNT) composite (NVPF@rGO/CNT) is successfully constructed. This composite features 0D Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>3</sub> nanoparticles are coated by a cross-linked 3D conductive network composed of 2D rGO and 1D CNT. Furthermore, the intrinsic Na<sup>+</sup> storage mechanism of NVPF@rGO/CNT through comprehensive characterizations is unveiled. The synthesized NVPF@rGO/CNT exhibits fast ionic/electronic transport and excellent structural stability within wide working temperatures (−40–50 °C), owing to the zero-strain NVPF and the coated rGO/CNT conductive network that reduces diffusion distance for ions and electrons. Moreover, the stable integration between NVPF and rGO/CNT enables outstanding structural stability to alleviate strain and stress induced during the cycle. Additionally, a practice full cell is assembled employing a hard carbon anode paired with an NVPF@rGO/CNT cathode, which provides a decent capacity of 105.2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.2 C, thereby attaining an ideal energy density of 242.7&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>. This work provides valuable insights into developing high-energy and power-density cathode materials for SIBs.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smtd202301277-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smtd202301277-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) are broadly used as commercial energy storage systems in various fields, including electric vehicles, electronic devices, and large-scale energy storage equipment.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smtd202301277-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202301277-bib-0001_R_d41595900e485" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Nat. Commun.">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the limited and unbalanced allocation of lithium resources poses an obstacle to the continued development and cost-effectiveness of future LIBs. As a result, there is an urgent need to develop alternative electrochemical energy storage systems to replace LIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smtd202301277-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202301277-bib-0002_R_d41595900e490" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Adv. Energy Mater. Infomat. Chem. Rec.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> SIBs have gained attention as a potential alternative to LIBs due to their low cost, rich resources, and wide distribution of sodium. However, on account of the larger radius of Na<sup>+</sup> (1.02 Å) compared to Li<sup>+</sup> (0.76 Å), the migration of Na<sup>+</sup> in SIBs electrode is hindered, which results in substandard rate performance and cycle stability for SIBs compared to LIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smtd202301277-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202301277-bib-0003_R_d41595900e502" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Nat. Rev. Mater. Battery Energy Battery Energy">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Similar to LIBs, cathode material accounts for the largest percentage of manufacturing costs and plays a crucial role in determining the performances of SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smtd202301277-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202301277-bib-0004_R_d41595900e507" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Energy Environ. Sci. Adv. Energy Mater.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For this reason, it is significant to develop high-specific-energy cathode materials with superior rate capability and remarkable cycling stability.</p>
         
         <p>From now on, layered transition metal oxides (LTMOs),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smtd202301277-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202301277-bib-0005_R_d41595900e515" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Nano Energy Adv. Energy Mater.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Prussian blue analogs (PBAs),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smtd202301277-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202301277-bib-0006_R_d41595900e520" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Battery Energy Joule">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and Polyanionic compounds (PACs)<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smtd202301277-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202301277-bib-0007_R_d41595900e525" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Chem. Soc. Rev. Small">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> are among the various classes of cathodes that have been extensively studied for practical application. However, LTMOs have poor cycle stability and PBAs show either low rate capability or poor cycling stability. In contrast, PACs have shown stable framework structures that provide thermodynamic stability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smtd202301277-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202301277-bib-0008_R_d41595900e531" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Adv. Energy Mater.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Currently, NASICON-type Polyanionic compound Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>3</sub> (NVPF) is considered one of the most potential cathode materials for SIBs. NVPF can deliver a high operating voltage of ≈3.7–4.1&nbsp;V (vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>) with a theoretical discharging capacity of 128&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup>. This corresponds to a superior energy density of ≈508&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> in theory and represents an important landmark in approaching the electrochemical capability of commercial LIB cathode materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smtd202301277-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202301277-bib-0009_R_d41595900e554" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - J. Power Sources Small">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Unfortunately, owing to its low electronic conductivity, it inevitably leads to inferior rate performance and poor cycling stability, thus impeding the practical application of NVPF.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smtd202301277-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202301277-bib-0010_R_d41595900e559" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Green Energy Environ.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To address this problem, extensive efforts have been made by researchers to explore various strategies that can facilitate both Na⁺ diffusion kinetics and electron transfer in SIBs. Among these strategies, integrating carbon materials with active materials has emerged as a straightforward and effective approach for improving the electrochemical performance of SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smtd202301277-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202301277-bib-0011_R_d41595900e564" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - J. Electron. Mater.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For instance, Liang et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smtd202301277-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202301277-bib-0012_R_d41595900e570" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Adv. Energy Mater.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> employed electrospinning to synthesize carbon-coated Mo-doped Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> core-shell nanowires (MNVP@C), which displayed a remarkable initial capacity of ≈116.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>. When it was at a high rate of 50 C, MNVP@C nanowires still demonstrated an impressive discharge capacity of ≈99.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>. Zhao et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smtd202301277-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202301277-bib-0013_R_d41595900e588" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - J. Mater. Chem.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> successfully integrated a robust reduced graphene oxide (rGO) with NaVPO<sub>4</sub>F, derived in a high capacity of 97.5&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 C. Liang et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smtd202301277-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202301277-bib-0014_R_d41595900e598" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Chem. Eng. J.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> developed polyacrylonitrile-derived carbon-coated Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>3</sub> multi-clustered hollow microspheres, delivering a high specific capacity of 116.2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.2 C. Despite these pioneering advances, each strategy often resulted in only limited improvements in the electrochemical properties of the electrodes. Moreover, the realization of satisfactory high-rate performance is challenging when using a single carbon layer, as the electrical conductivity between particles is generally not effectively enhanced under such circumstances. Besides, in most studies, additional conductive carbon black is added when preparing slurries containing composite materials that combine active materials and carbon materials. Excessive carbon components can reduce the proportion of active materials in the electrode, resulting in a decreased overall energy density and specific capacity for batteries. Therefore, achieving advanced cathodes with high specific energy and exceptionally long cycling lifespan remains a significantly challenging task.</p>
         
         <p>As is well known, the microstructure of electrode materials determines their electronic/ion conductivity, specific surface area, and structural stability, thereby exerting a significant impact on the electrochemical property of the electrode. Earlier studies have suggested that graphene/carbon nanotube cross-skeletons can form bridges between active material particles, effectively enhancing the electronic conductivity of electrodes and hence their electrochemical properties.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smtd202301277-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202301277-bib-0015_R_d41595900e619" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Battery Energy Nano Energy">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, to date, there has been limited research investigating the growth of the studies on Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>3</sub> on graphene/carbon nanotube conductive network and its underlying Na<sup>+</sup> ions storage mechanism at extreme operating environments such as low or high temperatures.</p>
         
         <p>In this work, we employed a facile self-assembly strategy to construct a 3D conductive network of NVPF@rGO/CNT with only 3.2% carbon content (rGO and CNT). Remarkably, even without the addition of a conductive agent, NVPF@rGO/CNT exhibited impressive electrochemical performance attributed to its structural advantages, resulting in both high specific capacity (≈121.7&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.5 C, 1 C═128&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>), ultrahigh-rate capability (≈101 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 60 C). The reaction kinetics were evaluated through electrochemical impedance spectrometry (EIS), cyclic voltammetry (CV), and galvanostatic intermittent titration techniques (GITT). Furthermore, ex situ XRD revealed the near zero-strain feature with a volume variation of only 0.4% during Na<sup>+</sup> ion insertion/extraction. We have also investigated the electrochemical properties of NVPF@rGO/CNT across a wide range of temperatures (−40–50&nbsp;°C). Finally, a sodium-ion full battery utilizing HC as the anode and NVPF@rGO/CNT as the cathode was assembled, demonstrating promising prospects for practical applications.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smtd202301277-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smtd202301277-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p><b>Figure</b> <a href="#smtd202301277-fig-0001">1</a> illustrates the synthetic procedure for NVPF@rGO/CNT. Initially, the NVPF precursor was synthesized using a microwave-assisted hydrothermal method. Subsequently, the NVPF precursor was combined with GO and CNT via a solvothermal method to produce the NVPF precursor@GO/CNT gel. Here, the self-assembly of rGO and CNT dispersions results in the formation of a 3D hybrid carbon architecture, which exhibits an open porous structure that facilitates electrolyte ion accessibility to the surface of NVPF. The final NVPF@rGO/CNT sample was obtained through calcination under an N<sub>2</sub> atmosphere. For comparison, a control experiment was conducted using identical protocols, but with an increased amount of GO replacing CNT, yielding the product NVPF@rGO. Additionally, pure NVPF was used as a control group and designated as NVPF. The sample of NVPF in situ growth on the surface of GO, which we previously reported, was also used as one of the control groups.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smtd202301277-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202301277-bib-0021_R_d41595900e663" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Further details can be found in the experimental section.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smtd202301277-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/a8da6c18-cbb6-4e56-9245-c061e57904dc/smtd202301277-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/a8da6c18-cbb6-4e56-9245-c061e57904dc/smtd202301277-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/3c15f3e8-19ee-4255-9caa-447be3ad8d46/smtd202301277-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/a8da6c18-cbb6-4e56-9245-c061e57904dc/smtd202301277-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smtd202301277-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmtd.202301277" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Schematic illustration for the synthetic procedure of NVPF@rGO/CNT.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The sample's morphological and crystallographic features were studied using SEM and TEM. SEM images of NVPF are presented in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smtd202301277-fig-0002">2a,b</a>. NVPF is a secondary particle composed of primary particles on a nanometer scale with a size of ≈300&nbsp;nm. SEM of NVPF@rGO/CNT and NVPF@rGO are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smtd202301277-fig-0002">2c–f</a>, respectively. The SEM images reveal that CNTs, rGOs, and NVPFs are tightly integrated with no evidence of agglomeration. In good agreement with these SEM observations, the low-magnification TEM image (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smtd202301277-fig-0002">2g</a>) shows uniformly dispersed CNTs and rGOs interlinking to establish a 3D network that can enable enhanced conductivities for speedy ion/electron diffusion while restraining the agglomeration of active material during frequent charge/discharge processes, thus benefit high-rate capabilities. High-resolution TEM (HRTEM) image of NVPF@rGO/CNT displays obvious lattice fringes with spacings of 0.32 and 0.45&nbsp;nm, indicating the crystal planes (2 2 0) and (2 0 0) of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>3</sub> respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smtd202301277-fig-0002">2h</a>). Moreover, the elemental mapping of the NVPF@rGO/CNT composite illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smtd202301277-fig-0002">2i</a> reveals a homogeneous distribution of Na, V, P, F, and C.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smtd202301277-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/932228cd-6741-42d8-8626-8c906b3c6d65/smtd202301277-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/932228cd-6741-42d8-8626-8c906b3c6d65/smtd202301277-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/bae9efdd-5c11-4d3f-94b3-28657cd47a60/smtd202301277-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/932228cd-6741-42d8-8626-8c906b3c6d65/smtd202301277-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smtd202301277-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmtd.202301277" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a,b) SEM images of the NVPF, c,d) SEM images of the NVPF@rGO/CNT, e,f) SEM images of the NVPF@rGO, g) TEM image of the NVPF@rGO/CNT, h) HRTEM image of the NVPF@rGO/CNT, and i) Elemental mapping images of the NFVP@rGO/CNT.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information) shows the XRD patterns of NVPF, NVPF@rGO, NVPF@rGO/CNT, and NVPF@rGO-600&nbsp;°C. The observed diffraction peaks of all samples were assigned unambiguously to a single phase of NVPF without any extra reflections (JCPDS data PDF#89-8485). <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smtd202301277-fig-0003">3a</a> illustrates the XRD curves with a magnified view, revealing that both NVPF@rGO/CNT and NVPF@rGO exhibit slight deviations in the diffraction peaks toward lower angles compared to NVPF, suggesting larger crystal plane spacing for the former two. To provide more details on the crystal structure, Rietveld refinements were performed for all three samples (NVPF@rGO/CNT, NVPF@rGO, and NVPF) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smtd202301277-fig-0003">3b</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information). The Rietveld refinement results demonstrate excellent agreement between the calculated and experimental data, with low R<sub>wp</sub> factors. The cell size and R<sub>wp</sub> factors for the Rietveld refinement results are presented in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information). Specifically, NVPF@rGO/CNT adopts a tetragonal structure with a space group of P42/mnm; lattice constants are determined to be a = b = 9.02822 Å, c = 10.64688 Å; V<sub>cell</sub> = 867.8 Å<sup>3</sup>. Compared with NVPF@rGO and NVPF, NVPF@rGO/CNT has significantly larger lattice parameters, which is conducive to endows enhancing Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics and structural stability (Tables <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2–S4</a>, Supporting Information). Raman spectroscopy was performed to evaluate all samples (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smtd202301277-fig-0003">3c</a>). Raman spectroscopy of NVPF displayed characteristic bands of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>3</sub> involving V─O and P─O stemming from V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>8</sub>F<sub>3</sub> unit. Two peaks at 940.9 with 1046.4 cm<sup>−1</sup> were caused by the symmetry of P─O stretching vibration with V─O/V─O─P anti-symmetric stretching vibration modes in [PO<sub>4</sub>]<sup>3−</sup> tetrahedron, respectively. The V─O stretching vibration was represented by the peak observed at 968.7&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup>, whereas the band ranging from 500 to 600 cm<sup>−1</sup> could be attributed to the coupling between P─O and V─O bend mode.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smtd202301277-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202301277-bib-0016_R_d41595900e805" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Adv. Mater. Chem. Eng. J. Adv. Energy Mater.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It should be noted that characteristic peaks of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>3</sub> are absent in Raman spectra of both NVPF@rGO/CNT and NVPF@rGO due to the signal being blocked by rGO and CNT indicating effective coating of NVPF by rGO and CNT. Raman spectra provided the fingerprints of carbon, which were revealed to be peaks at 1341.2 and 1595.1 cm<sup>−1</sup> corresponding to the typical D and G band structures of carbon, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smtd202301277-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202301277-bib-0017_R_d41595900e824" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Chem. Eng. J.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The relative intensity ratio (I<sub>D</sub>/I<sub>G</sub>) suggestive the level of graphitic ordering in carbon materials is computed as I<sub>D</sub>/I<sub>G</sub> = 0.87 for NVPF@rGO/CNT, which is obviously smaller than NVPF@rGO (I<sub>D</sub>/I<sub>G</sub> = 1.19), rendering high electronic conductivity for NVPF@rGO/CNT.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smtd202301277-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202301277-bib-0018_R_d41595900e842" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Adv. Mater.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smtd202301277-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/093326c8-7b26-4038-8e49-d7b68da2f1e7/smtd202301277-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/093326c8-7b26-4038-8e49-d7b68da2f1e7/smtd202301277-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/1521baeb-f04c-4d5a-be74-8b5e788531a9/smtd202301277-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/093326c8-7b26-4038-8e49-d7b68da2f1e7/smtd202301277-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smtd202301277-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmtd.202301277" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) XRD patterns, b) Rietveld refined XRD pattern of NVPF@rGO/CNT, c) Raman spectra curves, d) V 2p XPS spectra of NVPF@rGO/CNT, e) C 1s XPS spectra of NVPF@rGO/CNT, and f) TG-DTA curves of NVPF@rGO/CNT.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The XPS spectra confirm the attendance of C, Na, V, P, O, and F in the NVPF@rGO/CNT sample (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3a</a>, Supporting Information). The detailed spectra of each element species were also analyzed to determine their chemical states (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smtd202301277-fig-0003">3d,e</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3b–e</a>, Supporting Information). In the NVPF@rGO/CNT sample, the V 2p peak located at 523.6&nbsp;eV (V 2p<sub>1/2</sub>) and 516.5&nbsp;eV (V 2p<sub>3/2</sub>) represent V<sup>3+</sup> species.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smtd202301277-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202301277-bib-0019_R_d41595900e885" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Adv. Sci.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The C 1s spectrum displayed characteristic peaks of O─C═O (288.7&nbsp;eV), C─O (285.6&nbsp;eV), and C═C sp<sup>2</sup> (284.2&nbsp;eV).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smtd202301277-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202301277-bib-0020_R_d41595900e892" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - ACS Nano">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> NVPF@rGO/CNT and NVPF@rGO have a carbon content of 3.2% and 4%, respectively, as determined by the TG-DTA method. (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smtd202301277-fig-0003">3f</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information). The specific surface areas of NVPF@rGO/CNT, NVPF@rGO, and NVPF are 64.7, 68.8, and 34.6 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information). A larger specific surface area can shorten the diffusion distance of Na<sup>+</sup>, which is favorable for speeding the diffusion rate.</p>
         
         <p>The electrochemistry properties of various electrodes were measured at room temperature (25&nbsp;°C) using coin cells. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smtd202301277-fig-0004">4a</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a> (Supporting Information) display the GCD curves and rate performance of different electrodes. NVPF@rGO/CNT exhibits exceptional rate performance, delivering an average discharge capacity of 121.7, 120.6, 119.4, 117.4, 115.1, 111.5, and 108.5&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at rates of 0.5 C, 1 C, 2 C, 5 C, 10 C, 20 C, and 30 C respectively. Even at an ultrahigh rate of 60 C (charge/discharge within 1 min), a high discharge capacity of 101&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> is obtained. When the rate returns to 0.5 C from 60 C, the reversible capacity recovers to 120.8&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup>, indicating good reversibility for Na<sup>+</sup> storage. In contrast, NVPF@rGO exhibits inferior rate performance compared to NVPF@rGO/CNT. At rates of 0.5 C, 1 C, 2 C, 5 C, 10 C, 20 C, 30 C, and 60 C respectively, NVPF@rGO delivers an average discharge capacity of 115.8, 115.2, 113.8, 110.6, 107.4, 102.7, 99.6 and 92.3&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup>. The rate capability of NVPF is far worse than that of NVPF@rGO/CNT and NVPF@rGO, which delivers an average discharge capacity of only 95.5, 86.5, 78.2, 66.3, 57, 47.7, 41.5, and 30.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>. Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, (Supporting Information) shows charge/discharge curves for different electrodes at the ultrahigh current of 60 C. The curve for NVPF is severely distorted with almost no voltage plateau remaining; however, the curve for NVPF@rGO/CNT still exhibits good symmetry and displays a clear voltage plateau indicating fast Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics in this electrode. In previous research work,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smtd202301277-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202301277-bib-0021_R_d41595900e944" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> we utilized a microwave-assisted hydrothermal method to synthesize NVPF grown in situ on the surface of rGO (NVPF@rGO-600&nbsp;°C). It is worth noting that NVPF@rGO/CNT and NVPF@rGO electrodes show significant advantages in terms of rate performance compared to NVPF@rGO-600&nbsp;°C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smtd202301277-fig-0004">4a,b</a>), which is attributed to the optimization of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>3</sub> crystal structure during the solvothermal process. NVPF@rGO/CNT and NVPF@rGO electrodes have larger cell volumes (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information), which facilitate rapid diffusion of Na<sup>+</sup> in the host. XRD test results can strongly support this conclusion (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smtd202301277-fig-0003">3a</a>). In order to demonstrate the NVPF@rGO/CNT electrode's superior rate capability, a comparison was conducted with the performance of recently reported state-of-the-art Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>3</sub>-based electrodes (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smtd202301277-fig-0004">4b</a>; Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information), and it was evident that the NVPF@rGO/CNT held significant advantages.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smtd202301277-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202301277-bib-0009_R_d41595900e989" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - J. Power Sources Small">9</a>, <a href="#smtd202301277-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202301277-bib-0010_R_d41595900e989" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Green Energy Environ.">10</a>, <a href="#smtd202301277-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202301277-bib-0019_R_d41595900e989" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Adv. Sci.">19</a>, <a href="#smtd202301277-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202301277-bib-0022_R_d41595900e989" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Energy Storage Mater. InfoMat J. Energy Chem. Chem. Eng. J. Adv. Mater.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Cycling performances for all samples are presented in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smtd202301277-fig-0004">4c,d</a> at rates of 1 C and 10 C with excellent cyclic stability demonstrated by both NVPF@rGO/CNT and NVPF@rGO electrodes. After 500 cycles at 1 C, capacity retention for NVPF@rGO/CNT was 90.4% (107.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) with a decay rate of only 0.0192% per cycle; whereas for NVPF@rGO it was only slightly lower at 89.3% (98.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) with a decay rate of 0.0214% per cycle. In contrast to these two electrodes, structure deterioration was significant after only 400 cycles for NVPF. After 6000 cycles at a rate of 10 C, capacity retention for NVPF@rGO/CNT was 88.4% (100.9&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) with an inappreciable decay rate of only 0.00193% per cycle; whereas for NVPF@rGO it was significantly lower at 76.9% (82.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) with a decay rate of 0.00385% per cycle. In contrast to these two electrodes, the capacity for NVPF rapidly decreased after only 2000 cycles, reaching a specific capacity of only 27.7 mAh<sup>−1</sup> after 2800 cycles. The SEM images before and after 1000 cycles are shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a> (Supporting Information), it can be seen that the NVPF electrode has obvious cracking after the cycling test, while NVPF@rGO and NVPF@rGO/CNT electrodes still maintain good structural integrity. To show the performance advantage, the specific capacitance of the electrode was calculated based on the total mass of the binder, conductive component, and active material (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information). The NVPF@rGO/CNT electrode exhibited a specific capacity of 106 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.5 C, which is significantly higher than that of NVPF@rGO (100 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) and NVPF (76.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>). In particular, the NVPF@rGO/CNT electrode still exhibits a satisfactory specific capacity of 88 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at an ultrahigh rate of 60 C.</p>

            <figure class="figure" id="smtd202301277-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/7516496a-75ba-4b7b-a5cc-bfc8a44990b0/smtd202301277-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/7516496a-75ba-4b7b-a5cc-bfc8a44990b0/smtd202301277-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/8fbf077e-641b-4b71-b713-423beaf2b1e7/smtd202301277-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/7516496a-75ba-4b7b-a5cc-bfc8a44990b0/smtd202301277-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">

                  </div>

            </figure>
         </section>
         <div class="paragraph-element">To elucidate rate performance disparities among various cathode materials, we conducted CV tests on electrode materials at sweep rates ranging from 0.1 to 2&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup> (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#smtd202301277-fig-0005">5a</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>, Supporting Information). The two pairs of redox peaks (O/R and O’/R’) represent the reversible sodiation/desodiation of two Na<sup>+</sup> ions, which correspond well with the results of their GCD curves. Mater.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup>:
